201



103.2 W h kg−1 at 4000 W kg−1




Wen et al.159 synthesized NiCo2S4/MWCNTs hybrids via a simple solvothermal reaction and fabricated an asymmetrical supercapacitor. NiCo2S4 with 5 wt% MWCNT showed the highest specific capacitance of 2080 F g−1 at a current density of 1 A g−1 and, at higher current density of 20 A g−1, it delivered a capacitance of 1270 F g−1 with 85.7% capacitance retention after 5000 cycles. Fascinatingly, when fabricated as an asymmetrical supercapacitor with rGO, a high energy density of 51.8 W h kg−1 was obtained at a power density of 865 W kg−1. The enhanced performance is due to the presence of MWCNT which acts as a mini current collector and increase the flow of electrons. Recently, Beka et al.160 synthesised MWCNT/NiCo2S4 core/shell hybrid nanostructures via a two-step hydrothermal method. Specific capacitance of 1423 F g−1 and 1005 F g−1 was obtained at a current density of 2.5 A g−1 and 25 A g−1 respectively, whereas bare NiCo2S4 delivered a specific capacitance of 933 F g−1 at 2.5 A g−1 and 78 F g−1 at 25 A g−1. MWCNT/NiCo2S4 retained 81.4% of its initial capacitance after 4000 cycles. The MWCNT core acts as a pseudocapacitive active material with excellent conductivity and better mechanical stability.
Li et al.161 used carbon nanotubes (CNT) as a composite material to synthesize a CNT/NiCo2S4 composite via a simple chemical bath deposition method. Compared to bare NiCo2S4, the CNT/NiCo2S4 composite exhibited a better specific capacitance of 1537 F g−1 at 1 A g−1 and even at 100 A g−1 it retained a capacitance of 1200 F g−1. And, 91.5% of the initial capacitance was retained after 3000 charging–discharging cycles. Similarly, Lu et al.162 synthesised NiCo2S4/CNT via a simple reflux method. High specific capacitance of 2210 F g−1 and 1591 F g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1 and 60 A g−1. An asymmetrical device with activated carbon showed an energy density of 67.9 W h kg−1 at power density of 23.9 kW kg−1. The increase in capacitance in both the cases is due to CNT which was used as conductive matrix and increased the rate of electron transport. The role of CNT is to provide a highly conductive support; it provides a favourable pathway for improving conductivity and facilitates charge transfer within bulk electrode materials. Also, it provides good mechanical strength and enhances stability as explained by Lu et al.162
Ultrathin NiCo2S4 nano sheets were successfully grown on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) via a hydrothermal method as shown in Fig. 8(a). In the absence of rGO, NiCo2S4 formed a hollow spherical structure. A NiCo2S4/rGO hybrid electrode exhibited a specific capacitance of 1161 F g−1 at 5 A g−1 and even after 2000 cycles it retained 1108 F g−1 (4.5% loss) which was higher than a bare NiCo2S4 hollow structure (18% loss). Fig. 8 (3 and 4) shows the variation of specific capacitance at different current rates and their cyclic stability.106 In another case, Li et al.163 used reduced graphene oxide (rGO) as composite material to synthesize NiCo2S4/rGO hybrid and NiCo2S4 hollow spheres via a hydrothermal method. NiCo2S4/rGO hybrid delivered a specific capacitance of 1805 F g−1 at a current density of 0.5 A g−1. Thus, rGO provides a better electron transfer path and accounts for the increase in conductivity. 3D hierarchical architecture Ni–Co sulfide was successfully synthesized and modified using reduced graphene oxide by Gao et al.164 A modified electrode showed a specific capacitance of 1152 F g−1 at a current density of 0.5 A g−1 and reached 971 F g−1 at 20 A g−1 which was higher than unmodified Ni–Co sulfide. Reduced graphene oxide greatly increased the electrode conductivity and thereby enhanced the overall electrochemical performance. Cai et al.165 synthesised NiCo2S4 nanoparticles on rGO nanosheets via a one pot refluxing method (at a temp of 180 °C). The NiCo2S4/rGO composite delivered a specific capacitance of 1484 F g−1, whereas bare NiCo2S4 showed 835 F g−1 and 83% of initial capacitance was retained after 2000 cycles for this composite. Here, rGO serves as a perfect matrix for NiCo2S4 nanoparticles which improves the conductivity of NiCo2S4 and provides a pathway for fast charge transfer. In another approach, a one-step solvothermal method was employed for the synthesis of NiCo2S4 nanoparticles on graphene (GR) sheets without the use of surfactants. NiCo2S4/GR high specific capacitance of 1708 F g−1 was obtained at 1 A g−1 compared to bare NiCo2S4 (950 F g−1). An asymmetrical device fabricated with carbon black and NiCo2S4/GR delivered an energy density of 17 kW kg−1 at power density of 37.7 W h kg−1.166 Recently, Annamalai et al.167 reported a porous NiCo2S4 nanostructure on rGO synthesised via a facile synthesis method. Specific capacitance of 1498 F g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1. Wang et al.168 employed a one-step solvothermal method for synthesis of Ni–Co sulfide nanoparticles on graphene. The Ni–Co sulfide/graphene composite delivered a specific capacitance of 1437 F g−1 and 671 F g−1 at current density of 1 A g−1 and 50 A g−1 with capacitance retention of 92.5% after 5000 cycles. An asymmetrical device was constructed with rGO hydrogel which delivered an energy density of 39.5 W h kg−1 at a power density of 1778 W kg−1.
Xu et al.169 fabricated NiCo2S4@rGO@CNT as electrode material for a supercapacitor. A specific capacitance of 1242.5 F g−1 was obtained at a current density of 2 A g−1 which was found be higher than bare NiCo2S4. A unique carbon source with tubular architecture was derived from Juncus roenerianus and used as a substrate with inner and outer side coated with Ni–Co sulfide nanotubes. This unique architecture showed a specific capacitance of 747 F g−1 and 507.9 F g−1 at current density of 1 A g−1 and 20 A g−1. When investigated as an asymmetrical device with activated carbon, it delivered an energy density of 27.7 W h kg−1 at a power density of 236.3 W kg−1.170
Zou et al.171 synthesized carbon coated NiCo2S4 directly on graphene/Ni substrate via a hydrothermal method followed by sulfurization. Specific capacity of 253 mA h g−1 at 3 A g−1 was obtained and only loss of 6.1% of initial capacity was observed after 5000 cycles which was higher than NiCo2S4 without carbon coating (2.3 times). The carbon coating was proved to improve the electrochemical performance of the electrode material. Carbon@NiCo2S4 nanorods were synthesised by Li et al.172via a hydrothermal method using carbon supported nickel nanorods which act as surfactant and nickel source. Morphology of NiCo2S4 was controlled using H2O2 during hydrothermal treatment, which forms a porous structure. However, specific capacitance of 1455 F g−1 and 1262 F g−1 was obtained at 1 A g−1 and 20 A g−1 respectively with capacitance retention of 83% after 2000 cycles.
Unique 3D Ni–Co hydroxide/Ni–Co sulfide/graphene/Ni-foam was successfully fabricated by Tao et al.173 A Ni–Co hydroxide/Ni–Co sulfide/graphene electrode delivered a specific capacitance of 2001 F g−1 and retained 1646 F g−1 at high current density of 8 A g−1 in 3 M KOH electrolyte solution. A little addition of K3Fe(CN)6 to a KOH electrolyte provided an exceptional increase in the performance of its electrochemical properties and increased its specific capacitance to 6283 F g−1 at 6 A g−1. Recently, Luo et al.174 synthesised a NiCo2S4/Ni–Co double hydroxide via a vapor-phase hydrothermal method. Specific capacitance of 1765 F g−1 and 940 F g−1 was obtained at a current density of 1 A g−1 and 10 A g−1 with capacitance retention of 56% after 2000 cycles. The performance of these materials can be further enhanced by compositing with conductive materials like rGO, CNT, PPy, etc. This unique vapor-phase hydrothermal approach can be further extended for the synthesis of other binary metal sulfides such as ZnCo2S4, FeCo2S4, CuCo2S4, etc.
Li et al.175 synthesised a hierarchical NiCo2S4@Co(OH)2 nanotube array via a hydrothermal method followed by an electro-deposition method. As electrode material, it exhibited an areal capacitance of 9.6 F cm−2 and 6.4 F cm−2 at current density of 2 mA cm−2 and 32 mA cm−2, respectively. With activated carbon, an asymmetrical device delivered an energy density of 35.89 W h kg−1 at power density of 0.4 kW kg−1. The smart combination of NiCo2S4 and Co(OH)2 contribute to an overall boosted performance. In another attempt to enhance the performance of NiCo2S4, Nguyen et al.176 synthesised mesoporous NiCo2S4@Ni(OH)2 core sheet nanosheets on conductive carbon cloth substrate. High capacity of 209 mA h cm−2 was obtained at current density of 20 mA cm−2 with excellent capacity retention of 94.5% after 2000 cycles. Its high capacity can be attributed to its unique structure which provides high electro-active sites and the carbon cloth helps promote better conductivity.
Raj et al.177 fabricated a NiCo2O4/NiCo2S4 hybrid nanostructure on Ni foam via a facile ammonia evaporation technique. This hybrid structure exhibited a specific capacitance of 3671 F g−1 at 1.8 A g−1 and at 9 A g−1 it maintained a specific capacitance of 2767 F g−1 and 84% capacitance retention after 2000 cycles. NiCo2S4@NiCo2O4 nanocolumn arrays (NCAs) were fabricated on carbon textiles by Cheng et al.178 A specific capacitance of 2259 and 1139 F g−1 was obtained at current densities of 0.5 and 12.5 A g−1, respectively. A high energy density of 44.06 W h kg−1 was obtained at high power density of 6.4 kW kg−1 with capacitance retention of 92.5% after 6000 cycles.
Highly conductive NiCo2S4 single crystalline nanotubes arrays were successfully grown on flexible carbon fiber paper (FCP) and later a series of electro-active metal oxides were deposited onto it via electro-deposition. Out of various electro-active metal oxides like MnO2, FeOOH, and CoxNi(1−x)(OH)2, the CoxNi(1−x)(OH)2/NiCo2S4 composite exhibited highest specific capacitance of 2.9 F cm−2 at 4 mA cm−2 and better cycling stability (96% retention after 2000 cycles at 10 mA cm−2).179
Core–shell structured NiCo2S4 were successfully synthesized by tuning the ratios of Ni to Co via a simple two-step hydrothermal method which delivered a specific capacity of 155 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1 with good cyclic stability.180 In another case, hierarchical NiCo2S4@NiCoxSy core/shell nanoarrays were grown on carbon cloth which apparently showed a better performance than bare NiCo2S4. NiCoxSy was coated on NiCo2S4 nanotubes via an electro-deposition technique. An areal capacitance of 3.9 F cm−2 was obtained at a current density of 1 mA cm−2 and at 50 mA cm−2 it could deliver a capacitance of 2.9 F cm−2. The enhanced performance is mainly due to the presence of carbon cloth which acts as an efficient current collector and its unique core–shell structure provides more active sites for electrochemical reactions.181 In another report, Yang et al.182 reported hierarchical NiCo2S4@Ni(OH)2 core–shell hybrid nanosheet arrays on Ni foam delivered a specific capacitance of 240 mA h g−1 at 5 mA cm−2 with cycling stability of 81.4% over 2000 cycles. Ni(OH)2 nanosheet coating provides numerous accessible channels for ion diffusion. A unique hybrid nanostructure, when fabricated as an asymmetric supercapacitor device with activated carbon as negative electrode, showed an energy density of 53.3 W h kg−1 at a power density of 290 W kg−1. Yan et al.183 synthesized a NiCo2S4@PPy core–shell hetero-structure nanotube array on Ni foam and used it as electrode material without any conductive binder. Polypyrrole helps enhancement of capacitance as it contributes to pseudo-capacitance and also builds a better electron pathway with active material for enhanced electron transport. High areal capacitance of 9.8 F cm−2 was obtained at a current density of 5 mA cm−2 and when an asymmetric device was assembled with activated carbon, it delivered a high energy density of 34.6 W h kg−1 at a power density of 120.2 W kg−1. In 2017, Chen et al.184 fabricated NiCo2S4@PPy core–shell hybrid nanosheets on nickel foam via a hydrothermal method followed by electro-deposition. As electrode material, NiCo2S4@PPy delivered a high areal capacitance of 800 μA h cm−2 at current density of 5 mA cm−2 which was found to be twice that of pristine NiCo2S4 (383.9 μA h cm−2). An aqueous hybrid supercapacitor was assembled with NiCo2S4@PPy and activated carbon as electrodes, which delivered high energy and power density. Two such devices connected in series could light 38 LEDs effectively, which shows the potential of these nanostructures for practical applications.
To enhance the electrochemical performance of binary metal sulfides, researchers have attempted to incorporate metal oxides/sulfide in the binary metal sulfides matrix to come up with a unique design of an electrode material. Yang et al.185 constructed a unique architecture of MnO2 nano flake wrapped around NiCo2S4 nanotubes via a one-step hydrothermal method followed by carbonization treatment. The nanostructure delivered a specific capacitance of 1337 F g−1 at 2 A g−1 which was higher compared to bare NiCo2S4 (799 F g−1). Interestingly, 82% of capacitance was retained after 2000 cycles (50% for bare NiCo2S4). This enhanced performance of MnO2/NiCo2S4 is due to the outer MnO2 shell which helps to absorb cations on the electrode surface and undergoes an intercalation/de-intercalation process. Another similar approach was made by Xu et al.186 where hierarchical NiCo2S4@MnO2 core–shell nanosheets were grown on nickel foam. Areal capacitance of 2.6 F cm−2 was obtained at current density of 3 mA cm−2. Interestingly, 103.9% of capacitance was retained after 5000 cycles. Cyclic stability of NiCo2S4@MnO2 can be attributed to MnO2 acting as buffer for volume change during cycle tests. Fascinated by the architecture, Chen et al.187 developed unique NiCo2S4@MnO2 heterostructures via a mild hydrothermal method. Specific capacitance of 520 F g−1 was obtained at 1 A g−1 with capacitance retention of 86.8% after 2000 cycles. In another approach, He et al.188 synthesised unique NiCo2S4@Ni3S2 core–shell nanotubes via a hydrothermal method followed by an electro-deposition method. High capacitance of 4.25 C cm−2 and 3.12 C cm−2 was obtained at current density of 4 mA cm−2 and 40 mA cm−2, respectively. An asymmetrical device with NiCo2S4@Ni3S2 and 3D rGO aerogel as electrodes was fabricated which delivered an energy density of 32.75 W h kg−1 at power density of 0.36 kW kg−1. To further enhance the cycling stability of Ni–Co sulfides, Huang et al.189 synthesised NiCo2S4/NiO core–shell nanowires via a similar approach as Hu et al. It showed a specific capacity of 12.2 F cm−2 at current density of 1 mA cm−2 with excellent stability of 89% after 10000 cycles. This unique electrode material was evaluated for an asymmetrical device which showed a superior energy density of 30.38 W h kg−1 at a power density of 0.288 kW kg−1. Enhanced performance can be attributed to the unique NiCo2S4/NiO nanostructure. In yet another interesting case, Jia et al.190 demonstrated a unique hybrid of NiCo2S4/Fe2O3 nanoneedles as an electrode material for SC, which showed a specific capacitance of 352 F g−1 at current density of 5 mV s−1. An asymmetrical device was constructed with MnO2 and NiCo2S4/Fe2O3 as electrode material, which delivered an energy density of 2.29 mW h cm−2 at a power density of 196 mW cm−2.
Fu et al.191 fabricated CoS nanosheets via an electro-deposition technique on NiCo2S4 nanotubes synthesised by a hydrothermal method. This nanostructured architecture showed an areal capacitance of 4.7 F cm−2 at 5 mA cm−2 and 76.1% of initial capacitance was retained after 1500 cycles. CoS nanosheets coating on NiCo2S4 nanotubes provides a more effective pathway for electrons compared to bare NiCo2S4 nanotubes. Also, this nanostructured architecture with open and loose structures possesses enough space and surface for fast reversible faradaic reactions. Similarly, Zeng et al.192 tried a reverse architecture of NiCo2S4 nanosheets on CoS nanotubes synthesised on Ni foam. Wherein, areal capacitance of 7.6 F cm−2 was obtained at 5 mA cm−2 and 71.7% of initial capacitance was retained after 3000 cycles which was higher than previous results obtained by Fu et al. Here, CoS serves as a scaffold, providing a large surface for the growth of NiCo2S4 nanosheets and the growth of CoS on Ni foam provides robust adhesion which enhances the electrical conductivity. Very recently, hollow mesoporous NiCo2S4/Co9S8 submicro-spindles were successfully synthesised by Hou et al.193 Pseudo-capacitance of 749 F g−1 and 620 F g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1 and 15 A g−1. A fabricated asymmetrical device showed high energy density of 33.5 W h kg−1 at a power density of 150 W kg−1.
Chen et al.194 developed a distinctive approach, wherein they synthesised NiCo2S4 tubes on NiCo2O4 nanosheets and used it as electrode material. High specific capacitance of 4.3 F cm−1 was attained at 5 mA cm−1 with capacitance retention of 3.5 F cm−1 after 5000 cycles. When fabricated as an asymmetric supercapacitor it delivered an energy density of 24.9 W h kg−1 at power density of 334 W kg−1. NiCo2S4 nanotubes on the NiCo2S4 nanosheets possess a much higher specific surface, which helps in full utilization of electro-active material for energy storage and also provides good contact between the active material and electrolyte. In another attempt, Niu et al.195 synthesised a NiCo2S4 core with uniformly distributed Ni3V2O8 nanoparticles as a shell via a hydrothermal followed by a co-precipitation method. Specific capacitance of 512 C g−1 was obtained at 1 A g−1 and as an asymmetric supercapacitor it showed an energy density of 42.7 W h kg−1 at a power density of 200 W kg−1. Incorporation of Ni3V2O8 shell enhanced the capacity by providing highly electro-active sites.
In another case, the same strategy was employed to synthesize NiCo2S4–graphene–MoS2 which showed a specific capacitance of 1270 F g−1 at low current density of 1 A g−1. At a higher current density of 5 A g−1 it delivered a capacitance of 1002 F g−1 and excellent cycle stability of 94.8% retention after 4000 cycles.196 Tingting et al.197 synthesised NiCo2S4 on a nitrogen and sulphur functionalized graphene aerogel (N,S MGS) and used it as electrode material for a SC application. NiCo2S4/N,S MGA delivered a specific capacitance of 822 F g−1 and 556 F g−1 at current densities of 1 A g−1 and 10 A g−1, respectively.
Other than composite materials like rGO, CNT, MWCNT, metal hydroxide, metal oxide, and metal sulfide, recently Li et al.198 synthesised NiCo2S4 nanosheets on porous carbon nitride nanosheets and used them as electrode material for SC applications. Interestingly, specific capacity of 506 C g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1 with 100% capacitance retention after 1500 cycles. This suggests good cycle stability of a NiCo2S4/g-C3N4 electrode for practical applications. An asymmetric device was constructed with activated carbon which delivered an energy density of 16.7 W h kg−1 at power density of 200 W kg−1. This device fabricated with NiCo2S4/g-C3N4 could run a mini fan for about a minute, suggesting its importance in practical applications. The authors suggested that the enhanced performance can be attributed to unique sheet on sheet architecture which provides a short ion pathway. This result gives a platform for researches to explore the possibilities of metal nitrides as a composite material along with binary metal oxides and sulfides for various energy applications.
In another attempt to enhance the performance of NiCo2S4, Ketjen black was used as a composite material by Peng et al.199 NiCo2S4/Ketjen black nanocomposite delivered an areal specific capacity of 10.1 F cm−2 at current density of 10 mA cm−2 with 92% initial capacitance retention after 6000 cycles. High conductivity and surface area of Ketjen black along with NiCo2S4 nanostructures contribute to the overall enhanced performance. Similarly, Zhu et al.200 decorated acetylene black on NiCo2S4 hollow microspheres and used them as electrode material for an asymmetrical SC application which delivered an energy density of 24.7 W h kg−1 at power density of 428 W kg−1.
Wang et al.201 reported the use of NiCo2S4 hybridised with tryptophan functionalised graphene quantum dots (Trp-GQD) as electrode material for a SC application. Specific capacitance of 1453 F g−1 and 455 F g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1 and 20 A g−1, respectively. An asymmetrical device with NiCo2S4/Trp-GQD and activated carbon delivered an energy density of 157 W h kg−1 at power density of 800 W kg−1. A tryptophan functionalised graphene quantum dot hybrid enhanced the conductivity of NiCo2S4 and improved the mechanical strength of the binder layer.


3.3.2. CoNi2S4. 
Hu et al.112 synthesized CoNi2S4 nanosheets on Ni foam via a two-step hydrothermal method (Fig. 9). As electrode material, CoNi2S4 nanosheets delivered a high specific capacitance of 2906 F g−1 with higher areal capacitance of 6.3 F cm−2 at 5 mA cm−2. Most importantly, an asymmetric supercapacitor cell was set up using CoNi2S4 nanosheets arrays as the positive electrode and active carbon as the negative electrode; it exhibited a high energy density of 33.9 W h kg−1 at a power density of 409 W kg−1. Fig. 9c shows the FESEM images of the Ni–Co precursor and Ni–Co sulfide, respectively. Excellent performance can be attributed to its unique nanosheet-like structure and also good mechanical and electrical contact with high electrical conductivity and transport rate. CoNi2S4 nanosheets were successfully synthesized on Ni-foam using a one-step potentiostatic deposition method for the first time. A capacitance of 1932 F g−1 was obtained at 2 A g−1 and 1640 F g−1 at 20 A g−1. After 1000 cycles, 89.2% of initial capacitance was retained.211 In another case, nanostructured CoNi2S4 were successfully grown on a carbon substrate via a precursor transformation method. Different morphologies were obtained by varying the anions of Ni–Co salts. Material with NO3− as anion delivered a high capacitance of 2714 F g−1 at 1 A g−1.212
Wang et al.213 reported CoNi2S4 flower-like nanosheets on Ni foam and porous Ni via a one-step electro-deposition method and then used as a binder-free electrode material for a supercapacitor. It was found that the porous Ni/CoNi2S4 delivered a better specific areal capacitance of 1999 mF cm−2 compared to that of Ni foam/CoNi2S4 (1552 mF cm−2) and it also possessed a cyclic stability of almost 98.5% of its initial capacitance after 1500 cycles. The excellent performance of the porous Ni/CoNi2S4 integrated electrode was attributed to the high surface area associated with the CoNi2S4 flower-like nanosheets and also the porous nature of Ni skeleton current collectors. Du et al.214 synthesised CoNi2S4 nanoparticles via a solvothermal method with specific capacitance of 1636 F g−1 and 1351 F g−1 at current density of 1 A g−1 and 5 A g−1, respectively. An asymmetrical device was fabricated with activated carbon which showed an energy density of 36.7 W h kg−1 at power density of 7630 W kg−1. This asymmetrical device was successful in lighting up a LED and drive a rotating motor, which shows the potential of CoNi2S4 for real applications.
CoNi2S4/Graphene nanocomposites were successfully developed by Du et al.215 using a facile physical approach. A maximum specific capacitance of 2009 F g−1 was obtained at current density of 1 A g−1 and a specific capacitance of 755 F g−1 was retained at 4 A g−1 after 2000 cycles. The enhancement in its capacitance is due to the presence of graphene which acts as a conductive network and helps in efficient charge transport. The same group synthesized CoNi2S4 nanoparticles using a one-step solvothermal method which showed a specific capacitance of 1330 F g−1 at 1 mV s−1. After 2000 cycles it only retained 378 F g−1.216 Novel 3D flower-like CoNi2S4/CNT composites were synthesized by precursor transformation followed by an anion exchange reaction. As electrode material, it delivered a specific capacitance of 2094 F g−1 at 1 A g−1 and 83.4% of its initial capacitance was retained after 1000 cycles at 10 A g−1.217 Tang et al.218 used reduced graphene oxide as composite material for the synthesis of CoNi2S4/rGO using a solvo-thermal route without any template. A CoNi2S4/rGO nanocomposite showed a high capacitance of 1709 F g−1 at 0.5 A g−1 and at a high current rate of 10 A g−1, it retained capacitance of 1268 F g−1. In another case, Beka et al.219 reported the synthesis of flower-like CoNi2S4 on 3D graphene decorated Ni foam via a two-step hydrothermal method. It delivered a specific capacitance of 6.53 F cm−2 at a current density of 6 mA cm−2 with cycle stability of 85% after 3500 cycles. Its enhanced performance is due to the introduction of a graphene layer between Ni foam and CoNi2S4 which provides good mechanical contact and also increases conductivity. Zhao et al.220 reported a unique architecture of CoNi2S4 nanosheets with SiC nanowires on a carbon cloth as electrode material for a supercapacitor using a hydrothermal method which delivered a specific capacitance of 231 mA h g−1 at a current density of 2 A g−1. When fabricated as an asymmetrical capacitor with activated carbon on Ni foam as the negative electrode, it delivered a high energy density of 57.8 W h kg−1 with a power density of 1.6 kW kg−1. Tang et al.221 fabricated a flexible device using flexible nickel foam coated with CoNi2S4/NiSe material. Specific capacitance of 311 mF cm−2 and 136 mF cm−2 was obtained at a current density of 3 mA cm−2 and 15 mA cm−2 with 97% capacitance retention after 1000 cycles.
Recently, in 2017, He et al.208 synthesised bouquet-like NiCo2O4/CoNi2S4 nanoarrays via a hydrothermal method followed by an electro-deposition method. This unique architecture delivered a specific capacitance of 6.25 F cm−2 at 2 mA cm−2 which was found to be higher than the individual NiCo2O4 (1.08 F cm−2) and CoNi2S4 (1.25 F cm−2). And 88% of its initial capacitance was retained after 1000 cycles. This article suggested that the combination of oxides and sulfides can deliver a better performance compared to individual oxides and sulfides.


3.3.3. CuCo2S4. 
CuCo2S4 was synthesized via a solvothermal method using different solvents and then used as an electrode material for a supercapacitor for the first time by Tang et al.222 Materials obtained using glycerol displayed ultrahigh capacitance of 5030 F g−1 at 20 A g−1 and at higher current density of 70 A g−1, it retained a specific capacitance of 1365 F g−1. Similarly, mesoporous CuCo2S4 nanoparticles were successfully synthesized by Zhu et al.223via a simple solvothermal method using ethylene glycol. As obtained CuCo2S4 nanoparticles exhibited a better performance with a specific capacitance of 752 F g−1 at 2 A g−1 and 98.1% of initial capacitance after 5000 cycles. Recently, hierarchical CuCo2S4 hollow nanoneedle arrays were successfully synthesized on Ni foam using a facile template-free hydrothermal method and then used as binder-free electrodes. It exhibited a specific capacitance of 2163 F g−1 at 6 mA cm−2. The synthesized materials showed exceptional high cycle stability i.e., after 5000 cycles 98.7% of initial capacitance was obtained.224 Shen et al.225 constructed a 3D CuCo2S4/CNT/Graphene nanocomposite as electrode material for a supercapacitor which showed a specific capacitance of 504 F g−1 at a high current rate of 10 A g−1. Guo et al.226 synthesized CuCo2S4 on nitrogen doped graphene nanosheets via a one-step solvothermal method as shown in Fig. 12. As electrode material, it showed high specific capacitance of 1005 A g−1 at current density of 1 A g−1. Interestingly, at high current rate of 50 A g−1 it could still deliver a capacitance of 770 F g−1. The increase in its performance is mainly due to the presence of nitrogen doped graphene nanosheets which allow a large area of active sites and also act as a conductive network. In another attempt, Liu et al.227 synthesised a porous CuCo2S4/graphene composite using a simple hydrothermal method. As electrode material, CuCo2S4/graphene could deliver a capacitance of 425 F g−1 at 5 A g−1 and could retain 370 F g−1 at current density of 10 A g−1 with capacity retention of 83% after 1000 cycles. Recently, Xu et al.228 synthesised CuCo2S4via an improved solvothermal method. Specific capacitance of 449.6 F g−1 and 401 F g−1 was achieved at current densities of 1 A g−1 and 5 A g−1, respectively. CuCo2S4 with activated carbon was fabricated to form an asymmetrical device which showed an energy density of 15 W h kg−1 at power density of 422.5 W kg−1. These results suggest the potential of Cu–Co sulfide as electrode material for practical applications.
 Fig. 12  (a) Schematic of the solvothermal growth of the Co2CuS4/NG composite and its application in an asymmetric supercapacitor. (b) CV curves of NG, pure Co2CuS4, and the Co2CuS4/NG composite at 10 mV s−1. (c) Specific capacitance vs. current density. (d) Nyquist plots. (e) Cyclic stabilities at 10 A g−1 for 5000 cycles of NG, pure Co2CuS4, and the Co2CuS4/NG composite. (f) FE-SEM image of NG and Co2CuS4/NG composite (reproduced from ref. 226 with permission of The Royal Society of Chemistry226). 


3.3.4. Other binary metal sulfides. 
Tong et al.229 grew Zn–Co sulfide on carbon nanotubes/nitrogen-doped graphene (NGN/CNTs) via a hydrothermal method followed by a sulfidation process. The synthesized material was used as an electrode without any conductive binders; it delivered a high specific capacitance of 2484 F g−1 at 2 A g−1 and, interestingly, almost 0% fade was observed after 10000 sustaining charge/discharges at current density of 30 A g−1 which shows excellent cycling stability of the ternary sulfide. Yang et al.58 used a facile oil phase approach to synthesize Zn–Co sulfide and then applied it as electrode material for both a lithium-ion battery and supercapacitor. In the supercapacitor application, as electrode material it delivered a specific capacitance of 486 F g−1 at a current density of 2 A g−1. At a high current rate of 5 A g−1, 86.4% of initial capacitance was retained even after 2000 cycles.
Ramasamy et al.230 highlighted the importance of unique capacitance for intercalation of CuSbS2 in energy storage applications. As electrode material, a specific capacitance of 619, 697, and more than 1160 F g−1 resulted for LiOH, NaOH, and KOH electrolytes, respectively. It was found that lithium ions can be intercalated between the van der Waals layers without distorting the CuSbS2 structure which paves a way to explore the importance of CuSbS2 as electrode material in Li-ion battery applications.
CoMoS4 nanoparticles were successfully synthesized via a co-precipitation method and investigated as electrode material for a supercapacitor for the first time. A high specific capacitance of 415 F g−1 was obtained at a low current density of 0.5 A g−1 but, impressively, it showed an excellent cyclic stability with 100% capacitance retention after 10000 cycles at current density of 2 A g−1.231 To further enhance the performance, Xu et al.232 explored the importance of CoMoS4 as electrode material for a supercapacitor. CoMoS4 was synthesized via a simple precipitation method which showed a capacitance of 661 F g−1 at 1 A g−1. The high capacitance of CoMoS4 may be attributed to the multiple oxidation states, the synergistic effect of presence of Co and Mo, and high conductivity of sulfides. Yang et al.233 synthesized Co3S4/CoMo2S4 (CMS) ultrathin nanosheets on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) via a one-step hydrothermal method and then used them as electrode material for a supercapacitor. It delivered a specific capacitance of 1458 F g−1 at a current density of 1 A g−1 and favourable cycling stability of 97% after 2000 cycles. When fabricated as an asymmetric supercapacitor it exhibited a maximum energy density of 33 W h kg−1 at a power density of 0.85 kW kg−1.
Liu et al.234 studied the electrochemical properties of flower-like Mn–Co oxysulfide nanoparticles prepared by a facile hydrothermal method. Impressively, as electrode material it exhibited a high specific capacitance of 490 C g−1 at 2 A g−1 which was far better than that of Mn–Co oxides. After 3000 cycles, 86.5% of initial capacitance was retained at 20 A g−1 which shows good cycling stability. Later, Pramanik et al.235 synthesized carbon doped MnCo2S4 micro-cubes on Ni-foam via a solvothermal method. High specific capacitance of 1892 C g−1 was obtained at a current density of 5 A g−1 and 91% of capacitance was retained after 10000 cycles. Recently, Sahoo et al.236 reported porous manganese–cobalt–sulfide on Ni foam using an electro-deposition method. It exhibited excellent pseudo-capacitive behaviour with specific capacitance of 2421 F g−1 at current density of 1 A g−1 and even at 8 A g−1 it could still deliver a capacitance of 2000 F g−1.
Kumar et al.237 reported NiV2S4 nanosheet arrays grown on Ni foam via an anion exchange reaction and its use as electrode material for an asymmetrical capacitor. As electrode material it delivered a specific capacitance of 639 C g−1 at 2 mA cm−2 with capacitance retention of 90.7% at 30 mA cm−2 after 2000 cycles. Enhanced performance of the sulfide is mainly due to its low electronegativity and larger atomic size of sulphur, which creates a robust structure and helps with better transport properties. When fabricated as an asymmetrical capacitor with activated carbon as the anode it showed an energy density of 45.1 W h kg−1 and power density of 240 W kg−1.
To enhance the stability ternary sulfides, Shen et al.238 demonstrated novel FeNi2S4–graphene–MoSe2 composite electrode materials for supercapacitor applications via a hydrothermal method. As electrode material, it delivered a specific capacitance of 1700 F g−1 at a current density of 2 A g−1. Interestingly, specific capacitance of 106% was retained after 4000 cycles which shows a better stability of the active material. In another strategy, Tang et al.239 modified FeNi2S4 with graphene and CNT to form a FeNi2S4/CNT/graphene nanocomposite which showed a specific capacitance of 725 F g−1 at high current density of 10 A g−1. The electrode showed a capacitance retention of 88% after 2500 cycles.
In 2016 for the first time, Tang et al.240 explored the importance of FeCo2S4 as an electrode material for SC. Square FeCo2S4 tubes were synthesised via a controlled sulfidation process which delivered a specific capacitance of 2411 F g−1 and 1742 F g−1 at current density of 5 mA cm−2 and 50 mA cm−2. An asymmetrical solid state device was constructed with FeCo2S4//3DPNG which showed an energy density of 76.1 W h kg−1 at power density of 755 W kg−1. The authors suggest the superior performance of FeCo2S4 can be attributed to a rich redox reaction due to variable vacancies of Fe element and also due to its unique morphology. Zhu et al.207 fabricated a flexible electrode using a silver sputtered textile and coated with FeCo2S4–NiCo2S4 composite. Specific capacitances of 1518 F g−1 and 1296 F g−1 were obtained at current densities of 5 mA cm−2 and 40 mA cm−2, respectively. All fabricated solid state symmetrical devices showed high energy density of 46 W h kg−1 at power density of 1070 W kg−1 with stability of 92% after 3000 cycles. Table 3 highlights various other binary metal sulfides and their electrochemical properties e.g., specific capacitance, rate performance, capacity retention, energy, and power density.

4. Binary and ternary metal sulfides as counter electrode material for solar cells
4.1. Introduction
Sunlight, being an abundant source of energy, is undoubtedly capable of providing infinite and sustainable economic growth with minimal impact on the environment. And, in this direction, photovoltaics (PV) has been a very promising strategy so far. Research on first generation solar cells (silicon based) has continuously evolved with a focus on materials that are cost effective and that can harness the complete range of the solar spectrum. But, theoretically, the maximum achievable efficiency in these cells is only up to 33.7%.244 Nevertheless, they are durable and reliable. The second generation solar cells with processed thin films, although they are less costly, cannot yet stretch their efficiencies too far. The third generation solar cells are based on multiple electron hole pair generation and can allow their efficiencies to go much farther. Possible inclusions in this category are dye sensitized solar cells (DSSCs), quantum dot sensitized solar cells (QDSSCs), and perovskite sensitized solar cells (PSSCs). Sensitizers in such cells, upon irradiation, absorb sunlight and generate electron hole pairs. The electron transport layer (ETL), usually a mesoporous oxide, accepts electrons from the sensitizers. The hole remains on the surface of the n-type absorber where the reduced specie of the electrolyte donates an electron to regenerate the sensitizer. The transported electrons and oxidized electrolyte species finally combine and complete the circuit at the counter electrode as shown in Fig. 13.
 Fig. 13  Working principle of DSSC. 
4.2. Dye sensitized solar cell (DSSC)
DSSC has many advantages like easy synthesis methods for electrode materials and easy fabrication of cells. DSSC, although comparatively economical, have a platinum (Pt) electrode and expensive dye molecules which make up for the large portion of the cost involved. The high cost and limited reserves of platinum has encouraged researchers to find alternate materials for counter electrodes.
There are enormous amounts of research being done to replace the platinum electrode.245–248 Development of Pt-free counter electrode (CE) catalysts can make DSSCs more competitive among various photovoltaic devices in terms of cost effectiveness. A good catalytic and highly conductive CE material can rapidly reduce the oxidised redox couple (I3−) and regenerate sufficient amounts of reductant (I−) in the electrolyte such that as soon as the electron reaches the CE, it could prevent electron recombination and reduce the loss of photoelectrons to a large extent (Fig. 13).
Grätzel et al.249 were the first of a few to report the possible use of graphitic carbon as counter electrode material but were able to reach an efficiency of only 6.7%. However, since Lee et al.250 in 2008 were successful in achieving 7.56% performance, the pace of research in this field has become rapid and constituents like carbon materials, organic polymers, metal carbides, nitrides, oxides, sulfides, phosphides, tellurides, selenides, multiple compounds, and composites have been explored as alternate counter electrode materials. Theerthagiri et al.247 in 2015, and Wu et al.248 in 2014 compressively surveyed these findings. Further, in 2016 Chen et al.246 narrowed his review to list the findings on various forms of carbon material like microporous carbon, graphene, nitrogen-doped graphene, single-walled carbon nanotubes, and multi-wall carbon nanotube being used as CE material for DSSC. Carbon materials were reported to be promising candidates because of their low cost, high electron conductivity, good catalytic activity, thermal stability, and high corrosion resistance. However, their reduction capability and electrical conductivity still can't match up to those of Pt. Many researchers have expressed the few challenges that exist in preparing and applying these various carbon CEs in DSSCs.
Sulfides and selenides have shown huge potential for being used as CE materials and a number of photocatalytic and photoactive systems have been produced from these. Sulfides, namely CoS, MoS, WS2, NiS, etc., have been synthesized by various techniques and used as CE materials. They have exhibited exceptional catalytic activities in catalysing I3− reduction. Some noteworthy contributions include the first use in 2009 of CoS being electrodeposited on ITO/polyethylene naphthalate substrate as CE to give much higher conversion efficiency than Pt.251 Also, MoS2 and WS2 have shown comparable, or much more, power conversion efficiencies over Pt–FTO due to good catalytic activity and low resistance to charge transfer and Pumera et al.252 comprehensively reviewed such reports. Stability and scale up challenges do exist. They suffer from inherent instabilities in presence of moisture. Such instabilities are not only present when using them as CE materials but also when being manufactured and processed. The last couple of years have witnessed a variety of binary and ternary chalcogenides being applied as CE materials. There are many reports on NiCo2S4, CoNi2S4, CoMoS4, NiMoS4, CulnS4, Cu2ZnSnS4, and Cu2ZnSnS4 as some alternative materials for Pt CE. Two different cations coexisting in a single crystal structure can facilitate the binary and ternary chalcogenides to have more complex functions and enrich redox reactions; these are highly promising for CE by future DSSC research. At this juncture, it seems timely to summarise the recent work, analyse a few developments, and suggest future prospects of how these materials influence PV technology. This review narrowly focuses on advances in the development of binary and ternary CE and their role in sensitised solar cells.

4.3.1. NiCo2S4. 
NiCo2S4 is widely used as CE material due to its high redox reaction capability. The distinct d-electronic configuration of Co(III) at the surface of NiCo2S4 has added to its excellent electrocatalytic properties. NiCo2S4 is expected to offer richer redox reactions due to the contributions from both nickel and cobalt ions. The tetrahedral and octahedral sites occupied by Ni2+ and Co3+ also provide more hopping of charges between Ni and Co cations compared to the single phase of metal sulfides.107 Various synthesis methods have been employed like hydrothermal, solvothermal, and electro-deposition methods and ion exchange reactions to improve overall performance. Most of them were either hydrothermally or solvothermally synthesized since they produce high-surface-area catalysts with a large number of active catalytic sites.
Lin et al.,254 for the first time in 2013, synthesized NiCo2S4 microspheres via a facile solvothermal method, followed by electrophoresis for deposition, to obtain a high transmittance (N75%) and excellent electro-catalytic activity with cell efficiency of 6.14%, and was comparable to that of conventional Pt CE. Fabrication of an electrode by electrophoretic deposition had numerous advantages: namely control of thickness, low process temperature, good rate of deposition with uniformity, and feasible large scale deposition without the use of any binders. Furthermore, Anuratha et al.255 used a simple one-step electrochemical deposition by a pulse reverse technique and obtained dendritic nanostructures. They possess short diffusion lengths and high specific surface areas of fractals and provide pathways for small molecules to penetrate through their branches and reach the internal catalyst surface sites. Shi et al.253 synthesised NiCo2S4 nanosheets on ITO substrate via low temperature hydrothermal method (Fig. 14).
 Fig. 14  (a) J–V curves of the DSSCs with NiCo2S4 NS and Pt CEs at 1-sun illumination. (b) CV curves for the NiCo2S4 nanosheet and Pt-based counter electrodes. (c) Nyquist plots of EIS for symmetric cells assembled with two identical counter electrodes at an open circuit voltage bias under dark condition. (d) SEM image showing large-scale and uniform growth. (e) Cross section of NiCo2S4 nanosheet film (reproduced from Shi et al. Nanoscale Res. Lett., 2014, 9, 608).253 
Banerjee et al.256 synthesized a nano array of needle-like nickel cobalt sulfide by sulfurization (using H2S) of NiCo2O4 for good power conversion efficiency of 6.6% when used as CE (Fig. 15). But the concern of toxic H2S utilisation made Khoo et al.257 prepare NiCo2S4 nanosheets by using structure-directing agents, hexamethylenetetramine (HMTA) and L-cysteine (L-Cys). Large exposed areas facilitated better electron transport and regeneration of I−/I3− to give efficiency (7.22%) much higher than Pt CE. Chi et al.258 tried to deposit these nanosheets on carbon fibres used as substrate. The large exposed electroactive area of nanosheets and void space (that allows redox ion diffusion) significantly enhances the electrocatalytic activity and electron transport, enabling a higher catalytic performance toward I−/I3− redox regeneration, in comparison to conventional Pt–FTO CE. Nanowire arrays of NiCo2S4 were directly fabricated on FTO substrate by an ion–exchange reaction by Liu et al.259 for CE application. The solution-based IER avoids use of high temperatures and toxic gas sources such as H2S during the synthesis process. The efficiency obtained was comparable to that of old Pt CE (5.92). An observable narrowing of Epp as shown in a CV graph implies its strong ability to reduce I3− to I−.
 Fig. 15  (a) Formation of NCS nanoneedles from precursors. Step I is the formation of nickel cobalt oxide hydroxide carbonate nanoneedles by a hydrothermal method. Step II is the formation of nickel cobalt oxide (NCO) nanoneedles by annealing in air for 2 hours. Step III is the sulphurization process for the formation of nickel cobalt sulfide (NCS) in H2S/Ar gas at 350 °C for 8 hours. (b) I–V data of Pt, NCS, and NCO counter electrodes. (c) Cyclic voltammetry analysis of Pt, NCS, and NCO counter electrodes. (d) Impedance data of Pt, NCS, and NCO counter electrodes (reproduced from ref. 256 with permission of The Royal Society of Chemistry256). 
In another case, Huang et al.261 obtained mirror-like nanoparticles of this sulfide and developed them in situ on FTO by a solvothermal method. Tafel polarization, CV, open-circuit voltage decay (OCVD), and impedance spectroscopy measurements demonstrated that the catalytic activities and conductivities of these Ni–Co–S CEs approached or exceeded those of Pt-pyrolysis CE. Even 1000 cycles of CV didn't alter the particle morphology, and also 98% of its overall performance was retained. Huo et al.262 in 2015 tried a composite of NiCo2S4, wherein flower-like microspheres of NiCo2S4/NiS were produced by a simple two-step hydrothermal method. And the combined effect exhibited a large specific surface area, high conductivity, and excellent electro-catalytic activity for the reduction of I3− to I− and was able to reach an efficiency of 8.8% compared to DSSCs with Pt CE (8.1%). It can hence be concluded that performance is closely related to the synthesis method that resulted in crystalline type, particle shape, size, and also on stoichiometry. These binary metal sulfides have much lower intrinsic conductivities compared to Pt. They suffer from resistive voltage loss when compared to Pt and hence, addition of conductive media overcomes this shortcoming. But the mechanism by which these carbonaceous materials enhance efficiency was rarely discussed and Lu et al.260 reported the bi-functional activity of rGO wherein it acts as both co-catalyst in accelerating the tri-iodide reduction and a promotor of conductivity to decrease series resistance of the CE. Decrease of charge transfer resistance was more significant than the series resistance when rGO was coupled with the sulfide (Fig. 16). Highly crystalline nickel cobalt sulfide (NCS) micro-particles were mixed with a small portion of reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nano-flakes. Krishnapriya et al.263 further substantiates the enhanced activity of NiCo2S4–graphene nanosheet hybrids due to better electronic conductivity and better adhesion caused by a synergistic coupling interaction (at the interfaces of NiCo2S4 and graphene nanosheets). It is important to mention that prospects of commercialization of NiCo2S4 have been demonstrated by Su et al.,264 in their very recently published work (Dec 2016) wherein a facile low cost solution method to synthesize large scale NiCo2S4 and dip coating to deposit them on FTO was well demonstrated with an efficiency beyond 8.5%. They further used a flexible substrate, namely indium doped tin oxide polyethylene naphthalate, which brings it much closer to a possibility of commercialization. Table 4 shows the literature on NiCo2S4 as counter electrode for DSSC.
 Fig. 16  (a) Imaginary configurations of NCS/rGO hybrid CE with low (2%) rGO content and high (10%) rGO content. (b) TEM images of NCS/rGO hybrid CE with low (2%) rGO content and NCS/rGO hybrid CE with high (10%) rGO content. (c) IV curves of DSSCs using PVP-Pt and NCS-based CEs. (d) Experimental Nyquist plots of the symmetric cells based on PVP-Pt and NCS/rGO-based CEs (reprinted from ref. 260, Copyright 2016, with permission from Elsevier260).

4.3.2. CoNi2S4 as counter electrode. 
Binary compounds not only exhibit more complex functions, but also allow their properties to be readily tuneable by changing the stoichiometry of their components. Although not much of a difference in electro-catalytic activity or overall performance was observed, one can find researchers preferring nickel cobalt sulfide over cobalt nickel sulfide.
Chen et al.266 synthesized different morphologies like nanoribbons and nanorods (using different nickel precursors like nickel(II) acetate and nickel(II) sulfate respectively) via a hydrothermal method and obtained efficiencies up to 7.03% for ribbons, unlike rods that delivered an efficiency of only 4.1%. Pt CE showed an efficiency of 6.45%. A high specific surface area of a CE provides more catalytic sites for I3 reduction, which is favourable for regeneration of a dye sensitizer, thus enhancing catalytic performance. The intrinsic properties of CoNi2S4 nanoribbons and their polymorph nature, wherein a wide exposure of crystal facets contributed to significant distinction of conversion efficiency between two one-dimensional CoNi2S4 nanostructures.
Shi et al.267 synthesized CoNi2S4 nanostructures on a FTO substrate via a simple hydrothermal method at different reaction times (20, 90, and 300 min) at 130 °C. The electro-catalytic property of materials deduced from CV studies showed similar activity as that of Pt CE. The samples obtained at 90 min delivered the best catalytic capability and the lowest charge-transfer resistance, which is due to their formation of proper film thickness. Overall an efficiency of 4.61% was achieved with CoNi2S4 as a CE which was found to be comparable with Pt CE (4.67%) (Fig. 17).
 Fig. 17  (a) CV curves of different counter electrodes. (b) Photocurrent density versus photovoltage curves of DSSCs based on different counter electrodes (reproduced by permission from ref. 267, Nature Publishing Group, Copyright 2015267). 


4.3.3. CulnS2. 
CuInS2 (CIS) had earlier been exploited as an absorber layer for applications with solar cells. Due to its high absorption coefficient, relatively ideal direct band gap (1.5 eV), good stability under light radiation, and easy conversion of n/p carrier type, researchers have studied its electro-catalytic activity with respect to use as CE in DSSCs.268 Yao et al.269 in 2013 synthesized, via solution method CuInS2 (CIS) nanocrystals, NCs free of surfactant by exchanging organic capping ligands with inorganic ions S2−. This enhanced the active surface area, inter-particle coupling, and charge transport and they were able to achieve efficiency up to 6.53% that was much higher than Pt CE. Considering the low charge mobility in CIS NPs coming from the random boundaries between NPs, Zhou et al.270 and Liu et al.271 in 2014 made micro and nano spheres of CuInS2 by solvo and hydrothermal methods, respectively and obtained their composites of rGO. As previously mentioned, rGO improves the surface area for enhanced capture of electrons from a redox couple, adhesion of CIS particles, and also visible light absorption. And rGO also facilitates decrease of light reflection from CE to photoanode and ensures better energy conversion efficiency. Chen et al.272 in 2015 used a unique synthesis technique wherein Cu ions were incorporated into InS2 nanoplates by a solution process to avoid selenization and ligand exchange. The content of copper added proportionately changed the bandgaps of the CuInS2 nanoplates and could be tuned from 1.45 to 1.19 eV. The template synthesis of 2D materials demonstrated here allows one to control the shape and composition and hence electrical optical properties of CIS. Its use as CE material gave an efficiency of 7.09%.
Other binary metal sulfides. 
Molybdenum or tungsten sulfides based catalysts have been largely used for hydrogen evolution and hydride sulfurization reactions by electro-catalysis. Ni or Co act as promoters and significantly enhance catalytic activity.273–275 Promoter atoms are located at edges of the MoS2 sheets in the form of so-called Ni(Co)–Mo–S structures. Kanatzidis et al.276 reported the synthesis of porous CoMoS4 and NiMoS4 but Zheng et al.277 tried it as a CE in DSSC. They showed a better electro-catalytic property with an overall efficiency of 4.96% and 5.27%, respectively. Interestingly, addition of graphite enhances the cathodic and anodic peak currents and peak separation between the anodic and cathodic peaks decreases. Efficiency increases to 7.31% and 7.14%, respectively and was comparable to that of Pt CE (7.46%). Although few have been explored, they have huge potential for further exploitation of their electro-catalytic properties. Table 5 provides the literature on other binary metal sulfides used as CE for DSSC.
