4.3.4. Cu2ZnSnS4. 
The ternary metal sulfide Cu2ZnSnS4 (CZTS) is the most extensively researched and widely used material for solar cell applications. CZTS is a non-toxic, earth abundant, and low cost material. In addition, it is a direct band gap material with a high absorption coefficient of 104 cm−1 in the visible light region. Also, the value of the CZTS band gap is affected by the molar composition of CZTS, and lies in a range of 1.4–1.7 eV.278,279
Xin et al.281 for the first time reported use of CZTS as a CE material prepared by a solution method, followed by selenization which doubled the efficiency to 7.36%. Fang et al. tried to obtain surfactant-free CZTS but was only able to reach an efficiency of 3.6%, much lower than Pt CE. The pace of research is more rapid since then with many researchers working on in situ deposition or direct deposition of CZTS on substrates. Dai et al.282 tried a simple and cost-effective technique for fabricating porous surfactant-free CZTS thin film (that showed quite low reflectivity and effective light trapping) on Mo flexible foils. Liu et al.283 used a low temp in situ solvothermal method to deposit CZTS nanospheres on FTO and Wozny et al.284 used pulse laser deposition to grow a nanoplate array of CZTS. Swami et al.285 used a spray technique followed by annealing in a N2 + H2S atmosphere (to passivate defects on the surface and reduce recombination) to directly synthesize CZTS on FTO, while Tong et al.286 obtained an ultrathin transparent film of CZTS by a sol gel technique. An DC magnetic sputtering method was employed by Fan et al.280 by varying the atomic concentration for the synthesis of CZTS (Fig. 18). Most recently, in 2016, Park et al.287 found a facile solution process with spin coating for large scale deposition of highly nanoporous CZTS. All these techniques reached efficiencies between 5.5% and 7.5%.
 Fig. 18  (a) Plane view FE-SEM images of CZTS CEs with various molar ratios. (b) AFM micrographs of CZTS CEs with various molarities (reproduced from ref. 280 with permission of The Royal Society of Chemistry280). 
Chen et al.288 have pioneered themselves in this field of CZTS materials being used as CE. They innovated a green method via a solvothermal technique and pre-treated with a layer of TiCl4 to obtain well adhered nanosheets and particles on the substrate; it possessed the advantages of chemical stability, easy deposition, and mechanical stability. They also experimented on rounded morphology of CZTS nanosheet networks,289 which were synthesized by a solvothermal method and fabricated by electro-deposition. The fundamental requirements, mechanical stability, and chemical stability towards the electrolyte were tested after 7 days. The efficiency and FF was retained by 98% and 97% of its initial values, respectively; a mechanical peel-off test showed that the CZTS film was still strongly bound to the substrate, and the efficiency retained 91% of its initial value. A reflective mirror was also used to improve the visible light utilization rate and efficiency was observed to increase from 6.24% to 7.12%. In 2016 they used a solvothermal method for an in situ deposition of a semi-transparent 2D leaf-like structure of CZTS on FTO.290 A detailed growth mechanism of leaf-like structures was discussed. The thin smooth edges of kesterite nanoplates curl up and become cambered to form leaf-like structures. Efficiencies up to 8.7% were reached with assistance of mirror reflection. Again in 2016, they proposed the use of a carbon cloth as flexible substrate. The good conducting framework, better catalytic activity, effective electron transport, and iodide specie diffusion gave a power conversion efficiency of 7.53%. A stable and longer duration life performance (after 7 days, a loss of ∼6% in the PCE value retaining ∼100% PCE after 300 cycle bending) were observed.291
Various morphology like nanofibers reported by Mali et al.292 by electro-spinning and nanorods, nanoflowers (Fig. 19), and spherical CZTS reported by Mokurala et al.293 clearly convey that exposed surface area, porosity, and pathway for redox specie diffusion play a key role in enhancing performance. With the two phases of CZTS, kesterite and wurtzite, performance evaluation was done by Kong et al.294 who reported that wurtzite resulted in a better efficiency of 6.85% unlike kesterite which reached an efficiency of 4.89% that was much lower than Pt CE (6.23%). The wurtzite phase of CZTS is metastable and possess a hexagonal crystal system. This structure is beneficial for catalysis and charge conductivity and shows a higher carrier concentration and lower resistivity and hence high electrical conductivity.
 Fig. 19  (a) Schematic representation of electrospinning set up for the synthesis of CZTS nanofibers using different PVP and CA polymers. (b) FESEM images of CZTS nanofibers synthesized in PVP solvent. (c) FESEM images of CZTS nanofibers synthesized in CA solvent. (d) Current–voltage characteristics of the as-prepared dye sensitized solar cells based on Pt, PVP-CZTS, and CA-CZTS counter electrodes. (e) Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of as-prepared dye sensitized solar cells based on Pt/FTO, PVP-CZTS/FTO, and CA-CZTS/FTO counter electrodes. (reprinted with permission from ref. 292. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society292). 
Bai et al.295 tried composites of CZTS with graphene and CNTs. Here also, the conducive properties of graphene, as discussed earlier, prominently contributed to enhance efficiency by over 40%. The effect of 1D conducting pathway, surface area, and porosity surpasses the charge transfer resistance offered by graphene over CNTs where CNTs exhibit a very low charge transfer resistance. However, enhancement in the CZTS-CNT composite was only 20% (Fig. 20).
 Fig. 20  (a) I–V characteristics of DSSCs based on various platinum, CZTS, and CZTS/CNT counter electrodes. (b) CZTS/CNT composite cross section image of CZTS/CNT film in inset (a and b are reprinted from ref. 297, Copyright 2016, with permission from Elsevier297). (c) Photocurrent–voltage characteristics of DSSCs using different counter electrodes. (d) SEM images of CZTS microspheres with inset HRTEM image of CZTS nanoparticles. (c and d are Reprinted from ref. 295, Copyright 2013, with permission from Elsevier295). 
Li et al.296 recently, in 2016, explained the role graphene in CZTS–graphene composites differently. In addition to graphene greatly speeding up the extraction of photoexcited electrons from the CZTS, the composite is believed to have utilized unabsorbed photons and produce electrons, effectively suppressing recombination; this was evidenced by the largest Jsc obtained. This allows enhanced capture from the solar spectrum.
In 2017 Liu et al.298 reported breakthrough research in CZTS. They reported the possible use of new CZTS derivatives as CE materials. A combined theoretical and experimental study of a strategy that exploits the substitutional incorporation of K, V, Ir, or Pt cations to enhance the catalytic performance of CZTS is discussed. K doping was reported earlier by Tong et al.299 for enhancing the (new 1 1 2) preferred orientation and reducing the second phase of ZnS in CZTS thin film. Tablero et al.300 substituted cations Ir and V to create mid-band states. Subsequently, different metal-substituted CZTS surfaces (metal = Ir, K, Pt, and V) were designed as CE materials. These studies revealed that when Cu2+ atoms in Cu2ZnSnS4 are replaced by V, the material produced shows a high catalytic activity for the reaction of I3−/I−. These results provide helpful information with regard to doping of CE materials for enhancing the performance of DSSCs.


4.3.4. Other ternary metal sulfides. 
Cu2FeSnS4 (CFTS) and Cu2ZnGeS4 (CZGS) are two other ternary metal sulfides which have been used as counter electrodes for DSSC. Park et al.301 reported CFTS as CE for DSSC and compared it with other sulfides like CuS, Cu2S, SnS2, CuSnS3, Cu2SnS3 and CZTS. It was observed that out of possible Cu and Sn based sulfides, CFTS showed a better performance when compared to Pt CE (7.1%); CFTS showed a photo conversion efficiency of 5.6%. It was interesting to see that it could deliver a better performance than CZTS.
In another case, Prabhakar et al.302 synthesized CFTS using spray pyrolysis followed by sulfurization at different temperatures (400, 500 and 600 °C) for use as CE for DSSC (Fig. 21). Interestingly, CE CFTS showed a power conversion efficiency of 8.03% which was higher than that of Pt CE. Enhanced performance is due to its good catalytic activity in an iodine/iodide electrolyte as shown in Fig. 21. Also, the Mott–Schottky heterojunction of PtCo–CZGS nano-particles designed by Huang et al.303 as CE material promotes the transfer of electrons from semiconducting CZGS to metallic PtCo, which speeds up the reduction of I3− to I− in dye-sensitized solar cells. They were able to achieve efficiency up to 8.12%.
 Fig. 21  (a) C–V curves of CFTS5 and Pt in an iodine/iodide electrolyte. (b) Nyquist plots of symmetric cells (CFTS5 and Pt) measured at a bias of 0.5 V; (inset) magnified plot between 10 and 50 ohm (reprinted with permission from ref. 302. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society302). 

5. Oxygen reduction/evolution reaction
5.1. Binary metal sulfides in ORR and OER
Oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER) are the two fundamental reactions that prominently occur in advanced metal-air batteries and fuel cells (FCs). With advancements in FCs and metal-air batteries, platinum and its alloys have been widely used as ORR and OER catalysts. Since then, exploring highly efficient catalysts with low cost has been extensively studied as an alternative to platinum. However, recently there have been several reports on catalyst-based OER (FCs) or ORR (water splitting). A catalyst which can undergo both OER and ORR plays an important role in rechargeable metal air batteries and is called a bi-functional catalyst. Herein, ORR and OER correspond to the discharge and charge processes, respectively. Recently, in 2016, Lee et al.305 reviewed recent advances and progress made in using bi-functional electro-catalysts for metal-air batteries. They covered all the aspects pertaining to ORR, OER, and basic mechanisms and literature to date. Our focus in this review is to highlight the importance of binary and ternary metal sulfides for ORR, OER, and their bi-functional catalytic properties. As discussed in previous sections, out of all binary metal sulfides, NiCo2S4 has been one of the most studied and largely explored materials in various sectors such as LIB, SC, and SCs. In the next section we discuss the work done on NiCo2S4 along with few other metal sulfides for OER and ORR.

5.1.1. NiCo2S4. 
NiCo2S4 synthesised for the first time for ORR/OER applications via a solvothermal method using EGylated DES composed of PEG 200 and thiourea,306 when used as an electro-catalyst possesses a good OER with an onset potential of 1.45 V and lower Tafel slope (64 mV dec−1). The improved OER performance of NiCo2S4 originated from its short diffusion route and fast transportation for active species and, interestingly, NiCo2S4 demonstrated long-term stability for almost thirty hours. Following a similar approach, Wu et al. synthesised NiCo2S4 on 3D graphene foam. Further sulfurization yielded a unique architecture with high surface area and excellent electrochemical properties. A prominent peak at −0.29 V was observed in an O2-saturated electrolyte solution which suggests the composite exhibits catalytic activity towards ORR. It was found that the composite favours a four-electron oxygen reduction pathway in the potential range of −0.45–0.65 V.307 On the other hand, the NiCo2S4/nitrogen and sulphur-functionalized multiple graphene aerogel (NiCo2S4/N,S-MGA) was constructed by Tingting et al.308 and used as electrode material for a supercapacitor and ORR. A high cathodic peak at −0.407 V was observed indicating excellent activity towards ORR due to the presence of N and S atoms in the architecture, which promoted the adsorption of O2 over the catalyst. Wu et al.309 developed a one pot hydrothermal approach for the synthesis of NiCo2S4 on reduced graphene oxide where Na2S was used as a sulfur source as well as a reducing agent.
Wu et al.310 synthesised NiCo2S4 on carbon support; it showed an onset potential of −0.02 V and a Tafel slope of 76.2 mV dec−1 which was close to that of the Pt/C catalyst. In another report, the same group utilised 3D nitrogen-doped carbon foam as a substrate for the synthesis of NiCo2S4 and NiCo2O4 nanowire arrays. Both electrodes showed a comparable catalytic activity with a four electron pathway over Pt/C.311 Zhang et al.312 synthesised NiCo2S4 microspheres via a facile one pot method and used it as a bifunctional catalyst for OER and ORR. NiCo2S4 microspheres exhibited an onset potential of −0.05 V with a peak potential at −0.22 V. It was observed that electrons transferred per O2 molecule were found to be 3.98 which was close to the theoretical value of 4. The authors suggested that the electrocatalytic property of NiCo2S4 originated from the unique d-electronic configuration of Co(III) at the surface. In another case, NiCo2S4 nanoparticles were grown on graphene via a solvothermal method with ethylene glycol as solvent and used as a bifunctional catalyst.313
Liu et al.314 investigated the theoretical and experimental importance of NiCo2S4@NiFe LDH/NF hetero-structures as a bi-functional catalyst for OER and HER. The OER catalyst showed an over potential of 201 mV at a current density of 60 mA cm−2 with a Tafel slope of 46.3 mV dec−1. In continuation, Liu et al.315 synthesised NiCo2S4 on carbon cloth and utilised it as a bi-functional catalyst for OER and HER which delivered an overpotential of 305 mV for hydrogen evolution and 340 mV for oxygen evolution. An alkaline water electrolyzer made from NiCo2S4/CC needs a voltage of 1.68 V for a 10 mA cm−2 water splitting current. These studies suggest a new platform to explore the use of binary metal sulfides as potential electrode material for full water splitting. Sivanantham et al.316 synthesised NiCo2S4 nanowire on Ni foam to yield a unique architecture and to act as a bifunctional catalyst for ORR and HER with an overpotential of 260 mV and 210 mV, respectively. Further, Wang et al.317 and Han et al.318 demonstrated the use of NiCo2S4 microspheres and NiCo2S4 with nitrogen-doped CNT as an effective bi-functional catalyst for metal/air batteries and zinc air batteries, respectively.


5.1.2. ZnIn2S4. 
Liu et al.319 synthesised a hetero-junction of ZnIn2S4 nanosheets/1D TiO2 nanorods via a hydrothermal method for water splitting applications. When used as a photo-electrode it showed an onset potential of −0.53 V with a photocurrent density of 1.2 mA cm−2 which was higher than the TiO2 nanorod (0.05 V and 0.4 mA cm−2). The negative shift in onset potential and increase in the photocurrent is due to a large accumulation of electrons and the electron–hole pairs being separated at the interface which decreases charge recombination. In another attempt, the same group developed a multi-junction with ZnIn2S4 nanosheet/TiO2 film/Si nanowire configuration with an onset potential of −0.33 V.320 Further, Liu et al.321 developed a novel TiO2/CdS/ZnIn2S4 nanorod-nanosheet array (NRSs) as a photo-catalyst which delivered a photocurrent density of 9.33 mA cm−2 at 0.53 V. This unique architecture possessed a large photo response spectrum to visible light with good band alignment; also a CdS buffer layer lowered recombination probability which contributed to the high photocurrent density.

5.2. Other binary metal sulfides
A few other binary metal sulfides including Co0.5Fe0.5S,322 Zn–Co–S,323 CoMoS4,324 FeNiS4,325,326 and CuCo2S4327 have been explored as prominent bi-catalysts for OER and ORR applications.
6. Hydrogen generation reaction
6.1. Binary metal sulfides for hydrogen evolution reactions
Hydrogen is a known environmental friendly fuel that carries the largest energy density. Potentials of hydrogen as an energy carrier can be utilized via a hydrogen evolution reaction (HER). A HER is the production of hydrogen through the process of water electrolysis where the evolution of hydrogen is possibly limited and is based on desorption of molecules coming from the cathode surface and this requires efficient HER catalysts.

6.1.1. ZnIn2S4. 
For the first time, Lei et al.328 investigated the importance of ZnIn2S4 as a potential photo-catalyst material for H2 evolution reactions. As an electrode material, ZnIn2S4 showed an activity of 57 μmol g−1 h−1 and was enhanced to 800 μmol g−1 h−1 using Pt as a co-catalyst in the first cycle. A templated-free approach was followed by Chai et al.329 for the synthesis of fluorinated ZnIn2S4 microspheres via a hydrothermal method. Tian et al.330 studied temperature dependence during the hydrothermal method used for the synthesis of ZnIn2S4. As the temperature was gradually increased, there was an increase in total pore volume, average pore diameter, and surface area which affected the catalytic behaviour. It was observed that ZnIn2S4 obtained at 160 °C showed a better H2 production of 248.9 mmol g−1 h−1. Li et al.331 deposited ZnIn2S4 thin film on indium tin oxide (ITO) using a spray pyrolysis technique. UV-visible absorption spectra of ZnIn2S4 thin film showed an optical band gap of 2.1 eV which is close enough for photo-electrochemical splitting of water (2.0 eV).332 ZnIn2S4 microspheres were synthesized by a template-free hydrothermal method which showed a hydrogen evolution rate of 842 μmol h−1 at pH 1.333 Song et al. studied the effect of concentrations of In on morphology and photocatalytic performance. Of all the different compositions, ZnIn2.3S4−y delivered a highest photocatalytic activity of 363 μmol g−1 h−1 which can be attributed to its large specific area and the crystallite size.334 Chen et al.335 synthesized ZnIn2S4 with hexagonal-cubic phase via a bio-inspired approach using hydrilla as a template with H2 evolution of 14.9 μmol h−1. Shen et al.336 used various solvents such as water, methanol, and ethylene glycol which resulted in different morphologies such as a flowering cherry-like microsphere and micro-clusters, respectively; the aqueous-assisted synthesis method showed a maximum activity of 27.3 μmol h−1 mainly due to a unique morphology and better crystallinity.
Chaudhari et al.337 demonstrated a conversion of harmful H2S gas into useful H2 under solar light using ZnIn2S4 as a catalyst. Morphology of ZnIn2S4 was controlled by the presence of trimethylamine to obtain nanoplates/nanostrips. Optical studies showed a band gap of 2.34–2.48 eV with hydrogen evolution of 5287 μmol h−1. In another report by Bai et al.,338 ZnIn2S4 microspheres with surface area of 165.4 m2 g−1 was synthesized by a cetylpyridinium bromide (CPBr) assisted hydrothermal method. It was observed that CPBr concentration and pH greatly affected the electro-catalytic activity. ZnIn2S4 obtained at pH 2 showed a higher activity of 1544.8 μmol h−1 g−1, attributed to its flower-like microsphere morphology with good pore structure which was helpful for e−–h+ transfer. Recently, a cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB)-assisted hydrothermal method was carried out by Shen et al. for synthesis of ZnIn2S4 which showed a hydrogen production of 112.45 mmol h−1 and 122.5 mmol h−1.339,340 Lately, Shang et al.341 used a polyol-mediated hot injection method for synthesis of ZnIn2S4 sub-microspheres. Using 1 wt% Pt loading as a co-catalyst, ZnIn2S4 showed a H2 evolution of 612 mmol h−1 g−1 which was higher than bare ZnIn2S4 (151.8 μmol h−1 g−1). Presence of platinum enhances electroactive sites and favours high H2 evolution rate. A heterojunction of cubic quantum dot/hexagonal microsphere ZnIn2S4 showed a catalytic activity of 114.2 μmol h−1.342
Effect of doping. 
Shen et al.343 prepared ZnIn2S4via a hydrothermal method and used it for photo-catalytic performance by doping with various compositions of copper (Cu) (0 wt% to 2 wt%). It was observed that 0.5 wt% of doped Cu showed the highest photo-catalytic activity with hydrogen evolution of 151.5 μmol h−1 under visible light. Hui et al.344 doped Co which displayed a high production of 200.5 mol h−1. Doping of Co replaces Zn2+ by Co2+ and results in the increase of d(006) space, which promotes photo-generated charge separation and showed enhanced photocatalytic activity. Jing et al.345 and Wu et al.346 doped different concentrations of Ni and Cr in ZnIn2S4 and studied their catalytic performance. Cr3+-doped ZnIn2S4 showed a good H2 evolution performance of 3115.2 μmol h−1 g−1. In another case, Tian et al.347 and Zhu et al.348 investigated the effect of doping La and rGO/La in ZnIn2S4 respectively. Doping has always been one of the most effective techniques to tune the performance of an electrode material. Tian et al.349 investigated the doping effect of rare earth (RE) ions such as La3+, Ce3+, Gd3+, Er3+, and Y3+ on the photocatalytic activity of ZnIn2S4. It was observed that with 2.0 wt% of Y, Gd, Er, Ce, and La doping showed increased hydrogen production efficiency by 46%, 53%, 61%, 69%, and 106%, respectively. In a similar report by Shen et al.350,351 alkaline-earth metals and transition metals such as Ca, Sr, Ba, and Cr, Mn, Fe, and Co were doped in ZnIn2S4via a microwave-assisted hydrothermal process. Ca-doped ZnIn2S4 showed a greater catalytic performance than pristine ZnIn2S4 due to suppressed charge recombination via band-to-band transition in Ca-doped ZnIn2S4.
Effect of graphene/carbon composite. 
Zhou et al.352 synthesized ZnIn2S4/rGO nanosheets via a controlled solvothermal method. It was observed that the surface area of n ZnIn2S4/rGO doubled to 92 m2 g−1 due to the presence of rGO. The amount of catalyst was a key factor for the catalytic performance; 0.050 g of ZnIn2S4/rGO showed a max rate of 81.6 mmol h−1 which decreased to 40.3 mmol h−1 for 0.1 g of catalyst. A microwave-assisted method was followed for the synthesis of flower-like rGO/ZnIn2S4 microspheres with good photocatalytic hydrogen evolution of 132.3 μmol h−1.353 Similarly, 3D ZnIn2S4/rGO heterostructures were synthesized via a hydrothermal method with hydrogen production of 6365 μmol h−1 with 0.2 g of catalyst.354 Presence of rGO helps in hole–electron separation and better conductivity, which leads to lower charge combination in the system. Similar results have been reported in Ye et al.355 and Chen et al.356 In another attempt, ZnIn2S4 was grown on carbon nanofibers to form a core–shell via a two-step method which showed a hydrogen evolution of 95 μmol h−1.357 Chai et al.358 prepared MWCNT/ZnIn2S4via a hydrothermal approach and investigated the photocatalytic behaviour for hydrogen production. With 3 wt% of MWCNT, it showed a better activity of 684 mmol h−1. The presence of MWCNT enhances the charge transfer and the resulting enhanced charge separation efficiency. Recently, in 2017, Xia et al.359 compared the effect of carbon quantum dots (1D), carbon nanotubes (2D), and reduced graphene oxide (3D) as a composite material with ZnIn2S4 for the photocatalytic H2 production reaction. Out of all composite materials, rGO/ZnIn2S4 showed a higher H2 production rate of 2640.8 mmol g−1 h−1 which is the highest reported so far. Higher photocatalytic activity of rGO/ZnIn2S4 was attributed to a large surface area which provides higher nucleation sites and larger space for charge-carrier transfer.
Effect of composite material. 
Shen et al.360 demonstrated a series of Pt-loaded metal sulfides/ZnIn2S4 as a photo-catalyst for the H2 evolution reaction. Wherein, various metal sulfides such as Ag2S, SnS, CoS, CuS, NiS, and MnS have been explored of which CuS/ZnIn2S4 showed a higher H2 evolution performance of 201.7 μmol h−1. Similarly, Lim et al.361 investigated the role of MoS2, CuS, and Ag2S as co-catalysts with ZnIn2S4 of which Mo2S/ZnIn2S4 showed a better catalytic activity. Ding et al.362 explored the use of MoS2 as a co-catalyst along with rGO which showed a hydrogen evolution rate of 1.62 mmol h−1 g−1. With similar architecture, Yuan et al.363 reported the generation of 4169 μmol h−1 g−1 hydrogen. Later, many reports used MoS2 as co-catalyst; namely, Chen et al.364 and Wei et al.365 reported H2 evolution rates of 8.047 mmol h−1 g−1 and 130 μmol h−1 which were 28 times and 9 times higher than bare ZnIn2S4, respectively. These reports strongly suggest that Mo2S is a potential co-catalyst for H2 evolution reactions.
In a two-step method, Wei et al.366 synthesized ZnIn2S4/NiS and used it as a photocatalyst. Studies showed that NiS plays a critical role of co-catalyst, which helps in directional migration of photo-excited electrons, and also acts as active sites for H2 evolution. Junctions between ZnIn2S4 and NiS played a role in high photocatalytic hydrogen evolution at a rate of 104.7 μmol h−1 which was higher than Pt/ZnIn2S4 (77.8 μmol h−1). ZnS-coated ZnIn2S4 synthesised by Li et al.367 showed photocatalytic hydrogen generation in the presence of glucose. Mei et al.368 synthesized In2S3/ZnIn2S4via an ion exchange reaction. It was observed that the photocatalytic performance was higher in composites, compared to their pristine forms. In another report, Ni(OH)2 was used as a co-catalyst with ZnIn2S4 microspheres which showed a H2 evolution of 8.35 mmol g−1 h−1; this was 18 times higher than bare ZnIn2S4. Use of Ni(OH)2 enhanced interfacial charge transfer suppressing the recombination of electron–hole pairs.369 The unique architecture of 3D CdS quantum dot/graphene/ZnIn2S4 obtained by Hou et al.370 enhanced light absorption ability and improved the separation of photo-generated carriers. In another approach, a CdS quantum dot-sensitized ZnFe2O4/ZnIn2S4 nanosheet hetero-junction showed hydrogen production rate of 79.0 μmol h−1.371 Liu et al.372 and Ding et al.373 synthesised ZnIn2S4–g-C3N4via a hydrothermal approach and microwave assisted methods, respectively. This unique hetero junction showed a hydrogen evolution rate of 953.5 mmol h−1 g−1 with 40 wt% of g-C3N4 and 387 μmol h−1 with 20 wt% of g-C3N4 in the composite respectively.


6.1.2. CdIn2S4. 
Bhirud et al.374 prepared CdIn2S4 and analysed effects of surfactant on its morphology and photocatalytic activity. Among a series of catalysts, polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP) assisted CdIn2S4 with bipyramid morphology showed an excellent photocatalytic activity of 3238 mmol h−1. CdIn2S4 with graphene composite successfully fabricated by Mahadadalkar et al.64 demonstrated H2 production with enhanced photocatalytic activity of 4495 μmol h−1. Further, as discussed in the previous section, ZnIn2S4 has been considered as a potential electrode material for H2 evolution reactions. Taking this into account, Yu et al.375 and Chen et al.376 synthesised PdS-loaded CdIn2S4/ZnIn2S4 and CuS/CdIn2S4/ZnIn2S4 composite material which showed a H2 evolution of 780 μmol h−1 and 358.4 μmol h−1 g−1, respectively. Recently, a hetero junction of CdS nanowire on CdIn2S4 nanosheets was successfully synthesised by Wang et al.377 and used as a photoelectrode which showed a photocurrent density of 2.85 mA cm−2 at 0 V vs. a reversible hydrogen electrode (RHE). In another attempt, CdIn2S4/g-C3N4 was fabricated by Chen et al.378 where g-C3N4 was wrapped onto CdIn2S4 nanosheets in which CdIn2S4 not only helped with faster charge separation but also significantly improved the photo-stability of CdIn2S4.


6.1.3. CaIn2S4. 
Ding et al.379 explored the photocatalytic activity of CaIn2S4. Optical studies revealed that CaIn2S4 possessed an optical band gap in the range from 1.68 to 1.74 eV with a max hydrogen evolution rate of 30.92 μmol h−1 g−1. The authors suggested that CaIn2S4 composed of In3+ ions in a d10 configuration may be the reason for achieving high photocatalytic activity. The same group also utilised bimetallic Au–Pt alloy as a co-catalyst with CaIn2S4, which showed an enhanced activity of 107.6 μmol h−1 g−1; this was higher than CaIn2S4, Au–CaIn2S4, and Pt–CaIn2S4.380 In another attempt to enhance the catalytic property, Xia et al.381 and Jiang et al.382 utilised CNT and as a composite material with CaIn2S4 and g-C3N4, respectively. Using CNT as a composite material enhanced crystallinity and specific surface area of CaIn2S4 which resulted in an H2 evolution rate of 23.4 μmol h−1 g−1. Whereas, using g-C3N4 a high H2 evolution rate of 102 μmol g−1 h−1 was attainable.


6.1.4. Other binary metal sulfides. 
There are fewer reports on binary metal sulfides used for HER, but limited studies showed greater potential to replace the expensive platinum electrode. Ma et al.383 synthesized NiCo2S4 on Ni foam with thioacetamide as sulphur source via a hydrothermal method and used it for the hydrogen evolution reaction. As an electrode material for the hydrogen evolution reaction, it showed a very low onset potential of 17 mV with a Tafel slope of 84.5 mV dec−1. In another case, Gu et al.384 used carbon nanofiber/CNT as a template for the synthesis of NiCo2S4. Further, a composite material was doped via a phosphidation reaction and their catalytic properties were studied. Phosphorous-doped NiCo2S4@CNT/CNF showed an ultra-low onset potential of 27 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2. Wang et al.385 synthesised CoNi2S4via a one pot hydrothermal method and studied the effect of temperature on photocatalytic activity. It was observed that the crystallite size increased from 9.3 nm to 14.3 nm from temperatures of 160 °C to 240 °C. A catalyst obtained at 220 °C showed overpotential of 255 mV at 10 mA cm−2 for the hydrogen evolution reaction. In a rare case, another important binary metal sulfide, CuCo2S4, was explored as a potential electrode material for the hydrogen evolution reaction by Ge et al.386 in 2016. The authors followed a one-step method to prepare CuCo2S4 and CoNi2S4 to get nanorods with a cluster-like morphology. As electrode material, CuCo2S4 and CoNi2S4 showed an overpotential of 111 mV and 135 mV with a Tafel slope of 53 mV dec−1 and 63 mV dec−1, respectively.

7. Synthesis methods
Synthesis methods play an important role in controlling structures and properties of desired materials. In all previous sections, morphologies of different electroactive materials were discussed contextually. In this section, we briefly discuss various synthesis methods which have been employed for the synthesis of binary metal sulfides such as hydrothermal, solvothermal, electro-deposition, electro-spinning, etc. (Fig. 22).
 Fig. 22  Synthesis methods. 
Hydrothermal methods are simple and widely used techniques for the synthesis of nanomaterials. Here, water acts as solvent which is heated in a closed vessel called an autoclave which can maintain the desired temperature and pressure. With water being abundant in nature, it possesses remarkable properties and acts as a catalyst for the formation of the desired product under hydrothermal conditions. Another important advantage of using water as a solvent is that it is volatile so it can be easily separated from the product.
Hydrothermal methods have been widely used for the synthesis of various metal sulfides. Various research reports show different morphologies of the same material and also study their effects on performance. It has been observed that key parameters that result in unique morphologies include reaction temperature and the surfactant used. Hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) and hexamethylenetetramine (HTMA) are two examples of commonly used surfactants. For the synthesis of binary sulfides there are two main steps: first, is the formation of binary metal hydroxide/oxides; second, this is followed by sulfuration. Sulfuration is done to convert oxides to sulfides in the presence of sulfur sources such as sodium sulfide, thiourea, etc. In some cases, metal sulfides can be obtained in a one-step hydrothermal approach.
Many successful attempts have been made to synthesize binary metal sulfides directly on a conductive substrate such as nickel foam, carbon cloth, carbon fiber, etc. These act as a conductive matrix which enhances conductivity of the active material.
A solvothermal synthesis method is quite similar to a hydrothermal one, wherein the reaction medium is replaced by solvents such as ethanol, methanol, etc. Unique morphologies such as nanorods, micro flowers, and plate-like morphologies can be obtained by using different solvents and mixture of solvents. Similar to hydrothermal, in a solvothermal method the reaction mixture is heated in a closed vessel and heated to a temperature higher than the boiling points of the solvents. It has been observed that temperature and the surfactant plays a prominent role in the morphology of the materials produced.
Another widely used technique for the synthesis of metal oxides/sulfides is an electrodeposition method, where the active material can be directly grown on the desired substrate. An advantage of this approach is that it provides firm contact between the substrates and the active material. Nickel foam and carbon cloth are widely used as a conductive substrate for the growth of nanomaterials. Another advantage of using this technique is that the material deposited on the substrate can be directly used as an electrode material in various applications such as a lithium ion battery and a supercapacitor (which avoids the use of conductive additives).
Various other synthesis approaches such as precipitation, sol–gel, electrospinning, and solution based methods also have been explored for the synthesis of metal sulfides which are similar to synthesis of metal oxides.16
8. Summary/conclusion and future perspectives
Metal sulphides have been a Frontier material and are the focal point of investigations for energy applications. Binary metal sulfides (BMS) have been widely explored in various sectors such as lithium ion batteries, super-capacitors, hydrogen generation/evolutions, oxygen reduction reactions, oxygen evolution reactions, and solar cells etc.; even more so in the last five years. BMS as an electrode material for LIBs possesses high theoretical capacity, better conductivity, and rich redox ability that delivers a significant specific capacity. BMS in the form AB2S4 (where A, B = metal and A ≠ B) possesses better conductivity and redox ability and has huge potential as battery material. With a motivation from metal sulfides (MoS2, CuS, SnS, FeS2, etc.) being used as an electrode material for a Na-ion and Mg-ion battery (MoS2), attempts have been made to utilise binary metal sulfides for Na-ion battery applications. But, unfortunately, during the lithiation process intermediate polysulfide Li2Sx (2 < x < 8) forms, which easily reacts/dissolves in electrolyte and results in a decrease in reversible capacity. Aspects such as understanding the chemistry and reaction mechanisms of these materials in Li, Na, and Mg ion batteries are yet to be worked on and such information can enhance better utilization of BMS. Also, dynamics of the formation of intermediate polysulfides need to be studied. Compatible electrolytes with minimized shuttle effects can fairly enhance the performance of LIBs and also need to be studied.
Discussions on improvements in performance by tweaking the crystal structure and surface character have revealed a promising line of action for using BMS for energy applications. Some typical examples of enhanced performance were elaborately discussed. Increasing conductivity by incorporating various conductive carbons and conductive polymers such as reduced graphene oxide (rGO), carbon nano tubes (CNTs), graphene ribbons. Also, polyaniline, polypyrrole, and PEDOT poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene), respectively, have shown high promise for designing a very high performance electrode. Unique results have been discussed with support from characterization, thus providing new information on electron pathways. One can observe very few reports on study of the factors controlling ion dynamics of metal ions in new electrode systems. Understanding the interfacial and degradation mechanisms of these metal-ion batteries by in situ X-ray and neutron diffraction, X-ray absorption, impedance spectroscopy and microscopy techniques can open up new avenues for taking this research to an entirely new level. Optimisation and demonstration of full cell prototype metal-ion batteries using BMS and TMS materials was rarely found. Thus, it will be important to initiate large scale synthesis and construct operating systems so as to identify and work on their shortcomings.
Also, BMS and TMS are promising alternatives to expensive precious metal Pt as CE materials in DSSC applications. These CE materials are observed to be very promising and surpass carbon materials in many aspects to appropriately replace Pt CE. We note NCS and CZTS to be extensively researched and used as counter electrode materials. Various researchers have applied different synthetic strategies to obtain various morphologies so as to tune their electrochemical activity and performance. Porosity, crystallinity, stoichiometry, shape, size, and exposed surface area strongly influence the number of catalytic sites and regeneration of the electrolyte. Synthesis methods that avoid ligands and use of nontoxic components are preferred as a step towards green chemistry approaches. In situ depositions and fabrications that involve controlled thickness, low process temperatures, good deposition rates that result in short diffusion lengths, good electron transport, and suppression of charge recombination contribute to overall performance and are important parameters to be considered with respect to large scale applications. Substrates such as carbon cloth are very exciting material and can take the research of these CE materials far into the future. Use of graphene and CNTs as high surface conducting frameworks with BMS and assistance of reflective mirrors allows an enhanced utilization of photogenerated electrons. Doping CZTS with various cations for creation of mid band states and high catalytic activity has opened up a new era for obtaining various doped BMS and TMS as enriched CE materials for DSSCs.
Finally, the review allows promising solutions presented here to be broadly applied to other energy applications, majorly oxygen reduction, oxygen evolution, hydrogen generation, Li–air batteries, Na-ion batteries, and Mg-ion batteries.

Table Content:
Pranav Kulkarni	Mr Pranav Kulkarni completed his Bachelors of Science at Rani Chennamma University and Masters of Science in Physics at Christ University, Bangalore, India in 2015. Currently he is pursuing his Ph.D studies at the Centre for Nano and Material Science (CNMS), Jain University, Bangalore, India. Recently he worked as a visiting researcher at the National Institute of Education, Nanyang Technological University and the Department of Physics, National University of Singapore, Singapore. His research interests are focused on materials synthesis by molten salt, co-precipitation and other chemical methods, and evaluation of electrochemical properties for lithium batteries and supercapacitor applications.
S. K. Nataraj	Dr S. K. Nataraj, MRSC, is an associate professor at the CNMS, Jain University, Bangalore, India. Earlier he worked as a research associate at the University of Cambridge UK, Academia Sinica Taiwan and Chonnam National University South Korea. He is a member of the Royal Society of Chemistry. He has been granted 5 US/PCT patents, and published more than 40 peer reviewed research articles, reviews and 2 book chapters. He is also an editorial board member of three academic journals. His research interests are in the field of membrane-based separation processes, and sustainable energy materials for battery, supercapacitor and fuel cell applications.
R. Geetha Balakrishna	Prof. Geetha Balakrishna, presently the Director of the Centre for Nano and Material Sciences, Jain University, has focused her research on photochemistry and energy conversion materials. She works on perovskites, oxide/sulphides and colloidal quantum dots applicable to solar cells, water purification/disinfection/desalination and sensing technologies. She has guided and has been guiding many students on their research projects leading to doctoral and postdoctoral degrees. She is a fellow member of many scientific world bodies such as ACS, ANS, MRS, etc., and has been nominated the regional coordinator of the Global Materials Network from India and is the commission member of IUMRS.
D. H. Nagaraju	Dr Nagaraju D. H. has received M.Sc. in chemistry from Bangalore University, India in 2001 and Ph.D. from Raman Research Institute, India in 2011. After completing five years of postdoctoral work at National University of Singapore, Singapore, Linkoping University, Sweden and KAUST, KSA, he worked as a scientist and head of the department at Atotech India Pvt Ltd, India. Currently, he is an Associate Professor at the Centre for Nano and Materials Sciences, Jain University, India. His research interests are nanochemistry, electrochemistry, sensors, energy storage and conversion devices like Li-ion batteries, supercapacitors, electrocatalysis, fuel cells, hydrogen evolution catalysts, etc.
M. V. Reddy	Dr M. V. Reddy obtained his PhD (2003) from the University of Bordeaux, France. Since July 2003, he has worked on lithium battery (cathodes, anodes and electrolytes) and supercapacitor materials at the National University of Singapore. He has published 160 papers, with a h-index of 47, 8750 citations and has presented 70 talks. He serves as an editorial board member for Materials Research Bulletin and several open access journals, a committee member for MRS Singapore & IPS Singapore, and theme chair for Energy & Environment at the 2012 ICYRAM conference. He has trained many students, and won Outstanding SMP mentor (2010 to 2016) and Inspiring Research Mentor (2011 to 2017).
